About Aussie Grit&#13; In the high-stakes world of Formula One, only the fastest make it to the top. Few know this better than Australian Formula One legend Mark Webber. His career in F1 stretched an incredible 12 years, saw him earn 42 podium finishes and triumph in nine races, including twice-winning the crown jewel of F1, the Monaco Grand Prix.&#13; But the road to the top of F1 racing is long and full of deadly twists and strange turns. In his long-awaited Autobiography, Webber tells the incredible true story of the small town pizza delivery boy who climbed the apex of the worldâ€™s most dangerous sport. With startling candour, Webber takes us on a thrill ride through the highs and lows of his amazing career, detailing the personal struggles that drove him, revealing the truth at last behind his rivalry with Red Bull Racing teammate Sebastian Vettel, and allowing us access-all-areas into a very private life played out on the public stage.&#13; Filled with anecdotes about the great drivers, epic races and characters of&#13; Formula One, Mark Webber Aussie Grit is a fascinating account of a life in&#13; the cockpit of an F1 rocket and the inspiring, no-punches-pulled story of a great,&#13; gritty Australian.&#13;

 Foreword&#13; BY STEVE WAUGH AUSTRALIAN CRICKET CAPTAIN 1999â€“2004&#13; HOW COULD I SAY NO TO MARK WEBBER, ESPECIALLY WHEN he was raising funds for various childrenâ€™s cancer research charities? After all, what he wanted of me sounded like a once-in-a-lifetime adventure â€“ to be helicoptered into the untamed west coast of Tasmania to compete in the inaugural Mark Webber Challenge. Sure, it would take me out of my comfort zone, but I assumed the fitness required for this task was not beyond that of an Australian Test cricketer.&#13; How wrong was I?&#13; Twenty-four hours later, having somehow completed the Challenge, I woke in my Hobart hotel bed in readiness for a pre-game training session with the New South Wales Sheffield Shield team. The very instant I attempted to take my first steps for the day, I experienced a searing pain in my upper hamstrings that quickly cascaded into every nerve-ending in my body. I couldnâ€™t move beyond a geriatric hobble. I was in a world of hurt with no place to hide. But what at the time seemed like a recipe for disaster in fact became a godsend, because the next day I blazed my way to a swashbuckling century. My legs were still tortured and begging for mercy, so my only option was all-out attack, culminating in adventurous strokeplay and a stream of boundaries. I guess my forced plan B wasnâ€™t a bad option!&#13; Thatâ€™s the power of Mark Webber. He is a guy you want to be with in the trenches, a man who exudes calm yet has that down-to-earth character that every Aussie can relate to and respect. We have all admired his exploits on the track. Markâ€™s statistics are extraordinary: 12 years in Formula 1, 215 starts, 13 pole positions, 19 fastest laps, 42 podiums and nine wins. However, these remarkable achievements arenâ€™t the only reason Australians connect with Mark, for he is one of us, a man who has never forgotten his roots, stays true to himself, remains humble and always gives 100 per cent every time he suits up. Throughout his F1 career, we all felt like we knew him and as such we joined him on the journey, celebrating his successes and experiencing the pain of defeat. It was as if he was driving for us all, spreading goodwill and the spirit of Australia around the world. I was a big fan, following his progress from wherever I was in the world and regularly sending text messages, which he always replied to even when the issues of the day were complex and challenging.&#13; Mark has always made himself available to help out my charity, the Steve Waugh Foundation. A recent â€˜Day at the Racetrack with Mark Webberâ€™ event raised an incredible amount of money, such is his popularity.&#13; Iâ€™m sure there are many more chapters yet to be told in the racing career of Mark Webber, but some key constants remain. He is still the same guy who treats everyone with respect and courtesy, but underneath this disarming veil lies a passion and desire to be the best that he can be.&#13;

 Prologue&#13; â€˜MULTI 21â€™: A RAG TO A RED BULL&#13; WHEN THE TELEPHONE CALL CAME, MY FIRST REACTION WAS one of surprise.&#13; â€˜Wow,â€™ I thought, â€˜thatâ€™s a pretty big statement with 12 laps to go, given weâ€™re running this way round!â€™&#13; â€˜Telephone callâ€™ is the expression we use to describe radio communication from the pit wall to the cockpit. The statement being made over the radio was â€˜Multi 21â€™. Even in a sport where mixed messages are the order of the day, â€˜Multi 21â€™ was the clearest order we could have been given. Both Sebastian Vettel and I, in Red Bull Renaults #1 and #2 respectively, knew exactly what it meant: that our cars should finish the Malaysian Grand Prix, second round of the 2013 Formula One World Championship, in that order, #2 followed by #1, with me first and Sebastian second. Thatâ€™s how we were running at the time, with me leading my teammate late in the race and no threat from anyone else.&#13; Why was I surprised? Rarely had the call come when it was in my favour, thatâ€™s why. But this time the circumstances were very much in my favour: I had timed my crossover â€“ the moment at which to make the mandatory tyre change from one Pirelli compound to the other â€“ perfectly. Seb had got tangled up with the pursuing Mercedes and I found myself in clear air, so I was out in front.&#13; I was a lot less surprised by Sebastianâ€™s reaction to the â€˜Multi 21â€™ message.&#13; After the final stops, when he was cruising up behind me, I could see the â€˜letter-boxâ€™ opening on the rear wing of his car: he was using its Drag Reduction System (DRS) to increase his top speed. Straightaway I knew he was going against what the team had asked us to do. He was going to make it hard for me.&#13; Not for the first time another thought followed: â€˜How the f#*k are we, as a team, in this situation?â€™&#13; In hindsight I should have turned my engine back up and got into the fight, but there was so much going on in my head that it never occurred to me to do so. We came home first and second, but instead of finishing with the cars in #2 and #1 order, Vettel took the victory.&#13; The incident was the final nail in the coffin of my relationship with Red Bull Racing at management level. â€˜Multi 21â€™ was just one flashpoint in a sequence that began as far back as Istanbul in 2010. It was an important stage on my journey, but itâ€™s not the whole story.&#13; This book is.&#13;

 IN THE COCKPIT: A MARATHON IN SHOES TWO SIZES TOO SMALL&#13; ITâ€™S A CLOSED WORLD.&#13; All your senses are either severely limited or under unremitting assault. The space you occupy is small and bloody hot.&#13; The helmet is tight-fitting. Your view is confined to what you can see through the â€˜mailboxâ€™ opening in your helmet and in two postage-stamp-sized rear mirrors.&#13; And you are in this minor hell, all going well, for two hours. Youâ€™re in isolation, but itâ€™s far from splendid.&#13; The cockpit gradually becomes a lonely place as the clock ticks down to race start. In many ways itâ€™s similar to sitting in any racing car â€“ except itâ€™s a lot more claustrophobic.&#13; A Grand Prix car is built for speed, not comfort. The driver doesnâ€™t get into his car, he inserts himself into the cockpit as a living component of the machine. The cockpit is a compact place, especially for a driver of my dimensions. At around 184 centimetres and a fighting weight of 75 kilos I was never the ideal size or weight for a Grand Prix driver. There have been tall F1 drivers â€“ men like Dan Gurney in the old days, Gerhard Berger more recently, Jenson Button today â€“ but they are the exceptions who prove the rule. Better to be a jockey than a Michael Jordan.&#13; The space is really tight around your knees â€“ itâ€™s like sitting in an old-fashioned bathtub with your feet higher than your backside. The pedals are actually moulded around your racing boot, both for comfort and to counter excessive vibration: the last thing you want is for your foot to slip off the pedal at high speed in the middle of a race. The boots are thin-soled so you can feel everything and they are the one item Iâ€™m very fussy about â€“ once I break a pair in nicely they generally last me for a season.&#13; Helmets are different: I go through between four and seven. The exterior takes a bit of a hammering, especially if you are coming through from the back of the grid as the â€˜marblesâ€™ (small pieces of rubber coming off other carsâ€™ tyres) come pinging at you on the way through. I used to push my head back into the headrest a lot because that way I could move my head around in the helmet a bit. The interior is customised for each driver, although not the straps, and I found they used to sit a little too far rearwards for me. At fast tracks like Monza, Red Bull Chief Technical Officer Adrian Newey used to hate running any little windscreens in front of the cockpit because of the aerodynamic effect, and I had to open my mouth to counteract the helmet lift from the onrushing air. I would end up with two bruises under my neck.&#13; I had only one superstition: I always got into my car from the left, a hangover from my karting days â€“ if you got in on the right side of one of those you ended up with burns on your arm from the engine!&#13; But I did have a race-day routine. It began with a last visit to the treatment couch to have my hips and pelvis loosened up a little and my shoulders and neck rubbed.&#13; In F1 the pit lane opens to let the cars out on track for their final warm-up procedures half an hour before race start. At that stage I was always happy to talk to my partner, Ann, or Dad but people I didnâ€™t know were kept well away.&#13; Then it was time to hop in. Or, more accurately, step, slither and bump into your seat, which has been almost literally tailor-made to your unique dimensions. The driver is the biggest component in that cockpit: they make the seat up around him, starting from behind. It couldnâ€™t fit more snugly if it came from Savile Row.&#13; The seatbelts intensify that feeling. They come together in a six-pointed star at your centre: two of them come up through the groin area on either side of your manhood; and there are two lap straps and two shoulder straps, all designed to the nearest millimetre around your size. What most people donâ€™t realise is that if youâ€™re not careful the edges of the belts can fold or â€˜pinchâ€™ and when they do those edges can be quite sharp, especially around the more sensitive areas of a blokeâ€™s anatomy.&#13; With the help of one of your pit crew, the belts are pulled extremely tight, for two reasons: the first, obviously, is to hold you in place in the event of what we call a â€˜shuntâ€™; the second, less obviously, is to help eliminate the muscle fatigue that would come from trying to keep yourself properly in the seat without belts. You need to be a fixed component of the machine.&#13; How tight are the belts? Back in 2001, when I won the F3000 race at Imola, I was on painkillers to counter the effects of a broken rib caused by the extreme cornering loads in a recent F1 test in the Benetton-Renault.&#13; With less than 15 minutes to race start, I sometimes hop out again â€“ the reverse of bump-slither-step â€“ and attend to the physical necessities or, putting it another way, go for a pee at the last possible moment; and stretch the lower back and glutes one last time because they are going to be immobilised for the next couple of hours. Hop back in, check the belts and HANS device, the one that looks after the head and neck, get as comfortable as the car allows, connect the drink bottle.&#13; So: you are strapped into a space barely big enough to contain you in the first place. You can scarcely move, except for your feet, arms, hands. The headrest comes in pretty snugly around you; down goes the visor, the radio sits about 3 millimetres off your lips, earplugs in, drink tube coming in beside the radio â€¦ and the sensation of enclosure is heightened even more. With that comes stress and elevated heart rate, which goes up because of the pressure on your ribcage; you canâ€™t breathe normally as you would standing or running or riding a bike, youâ€™re in a quite different position.&#13; Donâ€™t focus too far ahead, just concentrate on getting the immediate things right. Talk to your race engineer: how strong is the wind out on the circuit? Whatâ€™s the track temperature? Have we got our plan right for controlling the first part of the race?&#13; As the countdown intensifies, all the people begin to melt away. Just moments before the start of the warm-up lap may be the first time this weekend I have seen, â€˜liveâ€™ as it were, the Ferrari or Mercedes alongside me on our row of the grid.&#13; A practice start at the end of pit lane was part of the pre-race routine; so was a full throttle check, always insisted on by engine-supplier Renault; then a radio check and an electronics check on the clutch. Coming through the grid was like threading the needle, manoeuvring the car through the literally hundreds of people â€“ pit crew, media, race officials, VIPs and countless hangers-on â€“ who manage to find their way out there.&#13; The warm-up lap is broken down into three sectors. Sector 1 is all about getting the engine cooled after sitting, sometimes for an alarmingly long time, waiting for the signal to go. Use high gears, say up to seventh, and sit at around 6000 revs if possible. Iâ€™m working the tyres, veering sharply from side to side to get them warm, but not overdoing the zig-zag stuff. â€˜Engine temp fine,â€™ crackles through my earplugs, so itâ€™s time to focus on the brakes. Now I can open the throttle and hit the brake pedal to warm them up without worrying about the effects of any overheating on the engine behind me. In Sector 3 Iâ€™m off the leash to do whatever I like on the engine front; now itâ€™s all about making sure brakes and tyres are up around their ideal working temperatures.&#13; F1 fans will be familiar with those sudden bursts of pace â€“ burn-outs â€“ from the drivers midway through their warm-up lap, and thatâ€™s another little bit of information to be retained. We used to be given documents telling us where we could start those burn-outs on the circuit in relation to visual cues like sponsor hoardings â€“ all very well if you could remember the right sponsor!&#13; Another factor in the equation: fuel. Is this a circuit where our fuel consumption is critical? Or, just as importantly, someone elseâ€™s? In my latter F1 days the Mercedes cars were known to be fuel-sensitive: if the two Red Bulls were on the front row we sometimes debated whether we should do an unusually fast formation lap to help set their nerves on edge, though in practice we never did. Pull up in my grid slot, select neutral â€¦ and wait for the moment of truth.&#13; I used to get a call from pit wall when the 17th of the 22 cars had reached its grid slot. Thatâ€™s when I selected first gear. Then I built the engine revs from the moment when the second of the five red lights on the gantry came on. Waiting for the lights to go out, the drivers feel their heart rate rise. Mine was less dramatic than many: with a resting heart rate in the low 40s when I was at peak fitness, it would climb to around 120 at a Grand Prix start and the maximum I ever recorded was 182. A big part of it is simple adrenaline; I could sound perfectly normal in conversation with my crew but my heart rate would be noticeably higher than before.&#13; The simple truth of a Grand Prix start is that you must react to the lights as best you can. People probably think youâ€™re trying to get a jump on everyone else by anticipating that moment â€“ but thatâ€™s a reasonably good way to get yourself penalised for a jump start. Itâ€™s hard to anticipate in any case because there is a random â€˜windowâ€™ of 3â€“5 seconds in which the lights will go out. Youâ€™ll be doing 100 kilometres an hour in less than three seconds and itâ€™s risky to get ahead of yourself.&#13; Raw acceleration, braking, cornering â€“ everything is on an extreme level. Even people who are used to racing at a high level in other categories struggle to totally calibrate with what a Formula 1 car can do. The more experience you gain, the slower things get, but the inputs â€“ the movements the driver makes on steering-wheel, throttle and so on â€“ are unbelievably fine.&#13; If the two Red Bulls were on the front row of the grid I would regularly lose 3 or 4 metres to Sebastian in the first couple of seconds of a race. We tried various techniques to help reaction time; I did a warm-up routine before getting into the car but I binned that idea because my reaction times were actually better on a lower heart-rate, not higher. Thatâ€™s to do with your unique physical make-up. In the days when they were teammates at Ferrari, Eddie Irvineâ€™s reaction times were markedly superior to Michael Schumacherâ€™s, and another Ferrari favourite, Felipe Massa, was always very quick. But bear in mind that we are talking about a range of around only two-hundredths of a second â€¦&#13; Part of my perceived problem with race starts in the later stages of my F1 career was not, as a lot of people probably thought, down to age, but my sensitivity to rubber! At any race start the driver wants to be as sensitive as he can to what happens when he drops the clutch and begins to feel the wheels move under him. Itâ€™s essential to eliminate or at least minimise wheel-spin â€“ and after the change of rules on F1 tyre supply I just couldnâ€™t â€˜feelâ€™ the Pirellis to the degree I should have done.&#13; So much information to process as the crucial moment approaches. Do we know the tyre temperatures? Whatâ€™s the grip level from the track like? It can vary dramatically depending on whether a driverâ€™s starting position is on the left or right side of the track. One will have had more rubber laid down on it during race weekend than the other as the cars follow the correct line, and that rubber will interact with your tyres to help â€˜launchâ€™ the car into motion.&#13; Whatâ€™s the clutchâ€™s â€˜biteâ€™ point, the moment at which the car comes alive? Back in my Formula Ford days it was pretty straightforward: 8000 revs, dump the clutch, bang, go! The technical sophistication of a Grand Prix car is light years away from that, but the reactions from the components governed by technology were never entirely predictable â€“ a bit like the driverâ€™s!&#13; And what about the track itself? They can be worlds apart: at Interlagos in Brazil, the start is uphill so the driver has to keep his foot on the brakes to stop rolling backwards; at Suzuka in Japan itâ€™s the exact opposite as the circuit plunges immediately downhill to that thrilling first corner and the car wants to start rolling forward.&#13; Then all hell is let loose on your eardrums, first by the sound of your engine screaming into racing mode. The first few seconds of a race â€“ often so crucial to what happens at the other end â€“ also depend on the track. Take Monaco: the run to the first corner, Ste DÃ©vote, is short, tense and incredibly tight; Monza is the opposite, with that long, long run down to the rightâ€“left chicane at the end of the straight. Getting the gear-shift points is vital: lights on the wheel and, if you have asked for it, a â€˜beepâ€™ will remind you to shift.&#13; A driver goes through so many emotions during the course of a race â€“ all your sensations are heightened â€“ so if someone goes off the track you can actually smell the cut grass before you go past the scene. Itâ€™s just as well, really: your eyes are not telling you all that much. The driverâ€™s eye-line is at knee level so the sensation of speed is greatly increased from where he is sitting, and the trajectory can be a little hard to pick out.&#13; Our vision â€“ already fairly tightly defined by our helmets â€“ is further reduced by the high sides of the cockpit with the padded insert to protect the driverâ€™s head in case of impact. Believe it or not, but when Iâ€™m in the cockpit I canâ€™t see my own front wing. And it only gets worse when it rains.&#13; Your visibility then is virtually nil, so you are making micro-decisions the whole time. I canâ€™t think of any sport, motorcycle racing excepted, where such focus is required, where your visibility is so severely tested at such speed. Every now and again you get a break in the level of spray, you catch a glimpse of a braking board or something else that will give you a context.&#13; Youâ€™re looking for hoardings or signs as markers to where exactly you might be on a 5-kilometre circuit. Your default vision has been pulled back so much closer to where you are sitting and believe me, pulling information from the side of the car rather than the front, working out to within 20 metres where you actually are, is mentally very draining. The racing lines change in the wet, too. More than ever, in these conditions, composure is whatâ€™s called for.&#13; Mental strength is certainly what you need when it comes to racing at a place like Monaco. But then there is no place remotely like Monaco. When I was leading there it was constant resetting, lap by lap: copy and paste what you did last time round, nudge your confidence level up over a two-to-three lap stretch; donâ€™t try to grab too much round there. At Mirabeau, for example, the downhill run followed by the right-hander that takes you towards the hairpin, you have to fight against the feeling that youâ€™re on the brakes too early. Jenson Button once asked me if I felt, as he said he did, that as the race went on the Monaco barriers actually closed in on you even more. My answer was a firm â€˜Noâ€™: those barriers donâ€™t move; itâ€™s always about you, your car and the track you are on.&#13; Our job is to keep the car on the absolute limit and get it through the corners as quickly as possible. Every F1 corner is a tightrope, with the driver working as hard as he can to balance the car and get a bit more out of it â€“ yet this animal underneath us is trying to pull our arms out of our sockets and our head off our shoulders.&#13; Corner entry is the crucial thing: hit the brakes at the right moment, hit them hard and ignore the deceleration forces â€“ up to five times your own body weight â€“ going through you.&#13; Next: turn into the corner itself. Some turn in early, others â€˜go deeperâ€™, then turn in later and more violently. Keep the car in line: donâ€™t put too much lock on, donâ€™t slide â€“ stay on that tightrope. Get to the apex, the real change of direction, and start thinking about getting out of the corner. Keep the beast in line before you release it â€¦ use all of the road and then some on exit â€¦ then get on the throttle. Focus, feeling and concentration, corner after corner, lap after lap.&#13; In fact you can get into that trance-like state that sporting people call â€˜the zoneâ€™. For a racing driver itâ€™s about flow and rhythm, your inputs allowing the car to weave its way around a circuit smoothly and consistently â€“ something you can gauge by your lap times. When I was â€˜in the zoneâ€™ I found myself thinking like a chess-player, several moves ahead; when the car was going through Casino Square I was already mentally at Mirabeau. There was that feeling of easy repeatability: every lap would be timed to within a tenth of a second of the one before or after. Rhythm â€¦ repetition.&#13; Itâ€™s so hard to shut down after a Grand Prix. Your ears feel as if youâ€™ve just got out of an aeroplane but then these things are fighter jets that stay on the ground, so in a sense you have. Youâ€™ve been fixed for so long at the centre of a storm of sound and vibration, your concentration levels have been phenomenally high as you process information coming at you and changing virtually with every metre of ground you have been covering. All you want to do is get out of there.&#13; Whatâ€™s it like to be in the cockpit of an F1 car?&#13; Itâ€™s like running a marathon in shoes two sizes too small.&#13;

 1&#13; No Wings, Learning to Fly: 1976â€“94&#13; I WAS AN ADDICT BEFORE I WAS 10.&#13; I simply couldnâ€™t get enough: Iâ€™d have my fix on a Sunday night, or Monday morning, and Iâ€™d keep coming back for another hit, and another one after that. It started in 1984, when I was just seven years old. That was the year Nigel Mansell should have won the Monaco Grand Prix for Lotus but crashed in the wet on the hill going up towards Casino Square. His accident left a young bloke called Ayrton Senna in with a great shout of winning his first Grand Prix in a Toleman Hart, of all things, but the race was stopped because of the rain and Alain Prost was declared the winner for McLaren on count-back instead. I remember it like yesterday. My drug, of course, was Formula 1.&#13; I was born on 27 August, 1976, midway through the memorable year when Britainâ€™s James Hunt and Austrian Niki Lauda went head-to-head for the world title. Their season-long duel, including Laudaâ€™s near-fatal crash in Germany, was the subject of the highly successful 2013 movie Rush. When I came on the scene the Webber clan was living in Queanbeyan (from the Aboriginal â€˜Quinbeanâ€™, meaning â€˜clear watersâ€™) in the Southern Tablelands of New South Wales, hard up against the border with the Australian Capital Territory. Itâ€™s best known for producing people who excelled in sport, like cricketer Brad Haddin, squash star Heather McKay and Rugby Union great David Campese.&#13; Queanbeyan had been the stamping ground for the Webber family for a couple of generations by the time I arrived. My paternal grandfather, Clive, was born in Balmain in Sydney and my grandmother on that side, Tryphosa â€“ Dad tells me itâ€™s a Biblical name but heâ€™s never come across it in his reading â€“ was from Cessnock in the Hunter region of New South Wales. Both of them moved to the Queanbeyan area early in life, before they knew each other. They married in Queanbeyan in 1941. My dad, Alan, came along in 1947 and he has one sister, Gwen.&#13; Clive was originally a wood merchant, back in the days when there was good business to be done delivering firewood. He delivered wood to Hotel Currajong, where then Prime Minister Ben Chifley spent a lot of time, and also supplied wood to Parliament House. When war broke out Clive went down to Sydney to enlist but was sent home when they realised they needed to hang on to the bloke who delivered wood to such important addresses. He continued as a wood carter until 1955, when he bought what became the family business, Bridge Motors, a Leyland dealership with two petrol bowsers on the footpath on the main street of Queanbeyan.&#13; My dad and mum met in the early sixties. My mum, Diane, was from a well-known local family, the Blewitts. Her dad, David George Blewitt â€“ â€˜DGâ€™ for short â€“ and her mum, Marie, were married in 1947, but Marie died of cancer at the age of 48 so my sister Leanne and I never knew her. Dad tells me Marie was a wonderful lady: they got along famously and she worked for him at one stage. DG had 2000 acres which my motherâ€™s sister Pam still owns and runs. Mum was at school with Dadâ€™s sister and often used to spend time at their home. Mum likes to say she couldnâ€™t stand Dad at first, but he insists that was only because he used to like watching the ABC, all the old English comedy shows he still enjoys, and she thought he was being a bit of a smartie-bum, as he puts it. She must have got over that because they started going out in 1968 and were married in 1971, on Dadâ€™s 24th birthday. As he likes to say, he â€˜Blewittâ€™ when he married her! My sister, Leanne, came along in 1974 and in 1976 I followed. I went to Isabella Street Primary and then Karabar High, both close to home. For many decades, Formula Ford was the worldwide entry-level category for aspiring racing drivers. It kicked off in the UK in 1967 and soon spread through Europe and across to the States. It reached Australia in 1970.&#13; The idea behind Formula Ford was to provide a basic single-seater car, each with the same engine â€“ they started with Ford Cortina GT power units â€“ and without wings in order to keep the design as simple as possible.  3&#13; A Wing and a Prayer&#13; RACING IN EUROPE WAS A REAL EYE-OPENER. We were all trying to forge a career, trying to keep the dream alive. I always had to work and all the guys I was instructing with at Brands Hatch were in the same boat. There were two areas I needed to address: my knowledge of my carâ€™s behaviour and how my own decisions could affect it, and my personal fitness.&#13; The key player in the first part of that learning process was the man who would be my 1998 teammate. Bernd Schneider was 12 years older than me. As a kid he was a karting star, winning the world junior title before moving up to single-seaters in the German F3 series. He took that title as well in his second year, 1987, and that earned him the big call-up to Formula 1 with Germanyâ€™s Zakspeed team. It was a difficult period for him, trying to qualify in a car that was always going to be at the back of the field â€“ especially when they made the disastrous switch to Yamaha engines. After two seasons with Zakspeed he took part in the opening round of the 1990 World Championship for Arrows and did well to bring that car home in 12th place.&#13; Bernd then found his niche in touring cars and sports cars. He raced with the well-known Joest team in a Porsche but by 1992 he was a Mercedes man, with several seasons in the DTM (the German Touring Car series) before he moved into the GT arena. By the time we got together in â€™98 Bernd Schneider was the reigning GT World Champion so he knew a thing or two about handling racing cars.&#13; Why did they partner me with Bernd? Norbert Haug explained later that there had been some other options, like putting two young guys up against a pairing of the regular Mercedes drivers, but they were always in favour of putting young blood and experience together. That had already happened when Bernd and Dario Franchitti raced together, and Norbert had confidence in Bernd because he was very open, a great team player and one who would do what he could to help the inexperienced drivers.&#13; Bernd Schneider was the first man to drive home to me how important car set-up was. Until I worked with him I had been pretty arrogant when it came to finding ways to improve the car. Iâ€™d always thought it was an excuse or a weakness if you couldnâ€™t drive around a problem or if you had to look at the telemetry, compare your teammateâ€™s lap times with your own and work out how he was achieving them. I always wanted to learn for myself and not show any weaknesses â€“ and suggesting that I needed things changed on the car seemed like a weakness to me. I felt that I should be able to get the job done with whatever equipment I had been given. I was so bloody pig-headed.&#13; That attitude didnâ€™t last long at Mercedes. They taught me that learning how to set the car up, how to make it go round a particular circuit in the most effective way, was a fundamental part of my profession. I had begun to realise how important it was, but Mercedes put a big emphasis on it and what they were telling me was spot-on: I didnâ€™t realise how much easier I could make my life simply by focusing on details.&#13; As well as helping me with the fine-tuning of the car, Bernd also taught me how to use it to better effect on my way round a track. Again it was a lesson that initially went against the Webber grain. Bernd was asking me to use the kerbs: jumping the different types of kerbs and chicanes a circuit might present me with and using them to keep the car on the trajectory I needed.&#13; I thought that looked bad: it was scrappy, it was hard on the cars and at times it could even give the impression that the driver was desperate, otherwise why would he resort to handling the car that way? Bernd cut through all that from the word go. He listened to me, then said simply: â€˜But your job is to get this thing round the track as quickly as possible.â€™ And he was right.&#13; You work hard to keep mistakes to a minimum, and making none at all is the aim, but Bernd was the past master at executing all the techniques that help you go faster. He left no stone unturned and thatâ€™s why he was phenomenal in touring cars and in sports cars. He was one of the fastest guys I ever drove with or raced against. Looking back, he probably went up that steep learning-curve in the early part of his career; he arrived at Formula 1 before he had completed the curve and learned a lot of those techniques too late. Thanks largely to him, I was fortunate enough to learn them in time.&#13; It would have been helpful to pick up that knowledge sooner, but I absorbed a lot from Bernd in that first year with him. In that crucial period of my career he was a real brother to me, my first real friend outside the UK. We both liked playing squash, we went running together, we obviously drove together in the same car and we would win many races.&#13; We had a very good relationship â€“ but that didnâ€™t mean he was a pushover. He wasnâ€™t a politician â€“ maybe thatâ€™s why he didnâ€™t do as well as he should have in Formula 1 â€“ but as a driving talent, he was phenomenal.&#13; Speaking of car control and self-control, Bernd acted like a brother on one particular occasion I will never forget. Thatâ€™s how they wanted us to turn up at the events, it was all part of the professionalism. On one particular day, though, I got myself into a scrape that looked anything but professional.&#13; It happened on the Frenchâ€“German border at SaarbrÃ¼cken. I was going to a Mercedes truck function, where Bernd was meeting me from Cologne. On this occasion I became confused at the tollbooth at SaarbrÃ¼cken and when I blasted out of there I got myself into the wrong lane. I was furious at myself for losing a whole 40 seconds, so I did a U-turn at the service station and headed back down onto the main motorway. The fact is I was going way too fast. Uncharted waters again, this particular motorway entry: down the bottom it really tightened up and it was a lot greasier than I expected. I wasnâ€™t hurt at all: I was cool when I got out and then about 30 seconds later the shock hit. It got worse when I realised the truck driver was out of his cab and intent on killing me. The driverâ€™s was the only straight door on the car. Berndâ€™s advice was brilliant: youâ€™ve got to go straight to the top because that way the story is unfiltered. Itâ€™s one of the most demanding street circuits a driver will ever face and the experience really opened my eyes: even though I finished fourth, it was the first time that I had felt so uncomfortable in a race car. Iâ€™d been going for my little jogs round Aylesbury and thought I was pretty fit. I was so keen to do well on the first day, but I could never repeat that effort.&#13; I couldnâ€™t fix the problem in a week, I just had to hug my teddy and get on with it. When I came back again these pricks were going to be in big trouble.&#13; *&#13; Itâ€™s funny how a couple of words can change the course of a career. Early in 1998, a year after I introduced myself to Norbert Haug, the man in charge of the Mercedes-Benz racing program, I found myself back at the Australian Grand Prix. In mid-year we would be taking the Mercedes name back to Le Mans, the world-famous endurance race in France, where itâ€™s fair to say the Mercedes marque had a chequered history.&#13; In 1955 a Mercedes was at the centre of the most shocking accident in motor-racing history, when Pierre Leveghâ€™s 300 SLR flew into the crowd opposite the main grandstand, leaving more than 80 spectators dead. That tragedy triggered Mercedes-Benzâ€™s withdrawal from motor sport. Not until 1988 did the three-pointed star return to the circuit in the department of La Sarthe; they won the 1989 event, missed 1990 and were expected to dominate the 1991 race when a young Michael Schumacher was part of the line-up. Instead they were caught out by a minor component failure on two of their cars and left with their tails between their legs.&#13; So the full-scale return of the Silver Arrows to Le Mans would obviously be the centrepiece of our working year. Iâ€™ve always been attracted to endurance racing and its twin challenge to man and machine. I was really looking forward to going there for the first time. There is a pre-qualifying event there a month or so ahead of the race weekend itself, with two six-hour sessions in which the drivers are required to reach certain qualifying times in order to book their place in the race. We only needed to qualify one car as Berndâ€™s achievement in winning the previous yearâ€™s GT title automatically brought qualification for Le Mans with it. Bernd did the donkey-work and set third-fastest time overall; I managed six laps, but the length of the circuit meant that I was in the cockpit for 40 minutes while I did them.&#13; Silverstone is another of our sportâ€™s most hallowed grounds, and soon after the buzz of seeing Le Mans came another high: the biggest win of my career to that point when Bernd and I turned the tables on the Ludwig/Zonta car at the British track in the second round of the championship. One of the great advantages of racing at Silverstone is that I could commute each day from home. About halfway to Silverstone there is a village called Whitchurch. It has one of those funny little English mini-roundabouts, and that was my undoing.&#13; Ann and I were in an E-Class Mercedes and as we approached the roundabout we came up behind a bloke who looked as if he was going to take the first exit and go left. Qualifying for the race proper is staged over four sessions on the Wednesday and Thursday of race week.&#13; I put in another 12 laps of the Le Mans track during qualifying, focusing on setting the car up for the race. Bernd did a phenomenal job: we went there not believing we could find the one-lap pace to grab pole, but Wednesday gave us a glimmer of hope and he pushed like hell to get there on Thursday. At Le Mans we were also allowed to use so-called driver aids such as traction control and an anti-lock braking system. It was even closer to a single-seater, in my view, than the car we had been sharing in the GT series proper. Bernd was used to those tools, thanks to his experience in the International Touring Car championship, and he was working hard to bring me up to speed not only with those systems but also with tyre management and fuel consumption. Becoming a Le Mans driver would have to wait another year.&#13; Meanwhile there was a GT championship to be won. My disappointment was tempered by happiness for Klaus, since that was his last year in racing, but I also felt it had been a little unfair on Bernd. His partner came from Formula Ford and F3, whereas Ricardo arrived as the new F3000 champion to partner Klaus and was already getting test drives in Formula 1. I could go toe-to-toe with them most times but sometimes I struggled, partly because it was Berndâ€™s car, basically, and he had it set up as he wanted it, and partly through sheer lack of experience.&#13; I didnâ€™t realise until a few years later how lucky I had been to see how Mercedes went about their racing: the discipline in every area was exemplary. Itâ€™s not such a big deal nowadays and it makes us laugh when so-called celebrities reveal theyâ€™re dating an older woman or younger man! At the end of February the FIA announced that the International Prototype Cup, the category the AMG Mercedes team was scheduled to take part in, had been cancelled. â€˜Lack of interest from other manufacturersâ€™ was the reason given, just as we were getting into our testing stride back in California.&#13; The dumping of the prototype series was cause for concern. It was a track where you could do a bit on the banking, but on the infield section you could test the engines at full noise, the gearbox, the tyre constructions, everything. A lot of people didnâ€™t know where we were testing as we were getting ready for this one crucial race. In our first test at Fontana we had three cars running; two of them failed, but the car I was in actually finished the test â€“ 1000 laps of the Fontana road course. The preparations, as you might expect, were so thorough that we actually had mobile phones in the cars, so the onlookers were taken aback to see the driver putting in an emergency call to the team back in the pits! But the mishap meant our car was pretty much out for the rest of the dayâ€™s running because it was in a difficult place for the recovery team to get at.&#13; What had just happened came as a complete surprise. We had been doing those 30-hour simulations and weâ€™d had to turn the car off because every other part of the team, the drivers and mechanics in particular, was destroyed. As a result we were pretty confident that the cars were going to be reliable for the race. Now, all of a sudden, there was this question mark.&#13; After pre-qualifying, there was major panic because we could see we just werenâ€™t quick enough. It was a very, very hotly contested year in terms of the intensity and pace of the race.&#13; We took ourselves off to Hockenheim and did some testing on the old circuit, the layout without the chicanes, which meant we were able to run at incredibly high pace, taking a bit of down-force off the car to help our top speed. Within the team our thinking was, â€˜Okay, letâ€™s put a bit more performance onto the straights with the aerodynamics and make the car a bit more slippery, and go there in better shape for the race.â€™ The critical area is the undertray at the front of the car.  7&#13; Base Camp at Everest&#13; RECENT YEARS HAVE SEEN A MARKED INCREASE IN THE number of drivers having to bring sponsorship money or some form of financial backing with them to F1 teams, even those that are not perennial back-markers. The first 10-day Challenge would take place in November after the completion of the Formula 1 season.&#13; The inspiration behind it was a familiar one for most top-flight sportsmen and sportswomen: the desire to give something back. They are generally fit people, they can take the strains and the pressures of the different countries we race in, and specialist trainers had always encouraged me not to have what they call too tight a pyramid. You want your physical fitness to be built on a good, broad foundation, and thatâ€™s what I had been working towards for years: going for a long paddle, going for a long run, sprint sessions, lifting weights, swimming, covering as many bases as I could. The Mark Webber Challenge encapsulated all of that, and it had two other key elements for me: the chance to work within a team, and the competitive urge to see how I might go against some pretty serious athletes.&#13; Being on the Challenge â€“ seven so far â€“ puts me in a completely different world from the one I normally operate in. In F1 you live in a fishbowl, where price means more than value, relationships are built on false foundations and the moral compass is sometimes distorted. The Challenge was the perfect antidote to what Ann often calls â€˜that Formula 1 lifeâ€™, a professional environment that became more and more disenchanting as the years went by. To me it is a happy coincidence that we announced the creation of the Mark Webber Challenge at the place where, six years later, I would rise to the challenge of being a Grand Prix winner.&#13; Although I wanted to test my own limits, the Mark Webber Challenge was not conceived as a competition, more as a personal test for anyone who wanted to join me in one of the most beautiful but also most daunting places on our planet. Since its inception, I believe it has become one of the most respected multi-sports challenges an athlete can face.&#13; The first Mark Webber Challenge was incredibly physical: big, big days, and 10 of them â€“ one of the hardest things Iâ€™ve done in my life, by a long way. One of the most pleasing things for me after the first Challenge was the reaction from Bernie Shrosbree, the rugged ex-marine who played such a part early on in my own quest for genuine fitness. It was Bernie who introduced me to the whole idea of multi-sports training; he came on my team for the first Challenge, when there were some demons in me asking, â€˜Well Mark, can you get through this thing yourself?â€™&#13; After those 10 days Bernie said to an interviewer, â€˜Mark is walking 10 feet taller than he was 10 days ago. Apparently his comment to Bernie was, â€˜Heâ€™s flipped over to pure leadership.â€™&#13; It was a relief to me that I had come through it, but the last thing anyone should think is that the event that carries my name is all about me. Itâ€™s about encouraging people to step outside their comfort zone and discover what they are capable of. Every participant has his or her story about why they are there in the first place â€“ it gives me goose bumps when I first hear those stories. Iâ€™m reminded of Wayne Bennettâ€™s memoir, The Man in the Mirror, one of the finest sports books you will ever read. The book and its author have been twin sources of inspiration to me for years. I believe many of the men and women who have come with us on the Challenge have passed that test â€“ even if they didnâ€™t think they would.&#13; *&#13; After the extreme high of the inaugural Mark Webber Challenge in Tasmania in November 2003, the start of the 2004 racing year brought me back to earth with a bump.  8&#13; â€˜This Canâ€™t be Happening â€¦â€™&#13; DURING MY TWO YEARS AT JAGUAR RACING ITS MARKETING guru, Nav Sidhu, came up with the idea for the team to generate some publicity by getting on the bandwagon with a couple of blockbuster movies which were just about to be released in the UK.  9&#13; Smile Back on the Dial: 2007&#13; ITâ€™S STRANGE, BUT THE YEARS THAT DEFINED MY F1 CAREER and gave me my greatest successes are the ones Iâ€™ve struggled to find the motivation to write about. I donâ€™t think itâ€™s because Iâ€™m disappointed how it panned out for me personally because Iâ€™ve never forgotten where I came from, or what I managed to achieve. I was disappointed to discover a darker side of the sport which I was unaware of when I was racing for lower-ranked teams. But when there are race wins and championships at stake and the big money that goes with them, you enter a world where you simply become a pawn in someone elseâ€™s game, where politics and hidden agendas are the order of the day. I remember Sir Frank Williams saying when I signed for his team that I was the most apolitical person he had ever met in F1. Iâ€™m old-fashioned; as a Grand Prix driver I loved to race and liked those races to be sprints from start to finish. In recent years, that component has been diluted almost to the point where the drivers are either vastly over-qualified for the job they have to do or the job has become so easy that anyone with half an idea could graduate to F1 with relative ease. Where once being granted an F1 super-licence was a privilege and something you aspired to â€“ you could only get one if you had finished in the top three of an FIA-affiliated championship â€“ in more recent years it seems you can apply for one on the back of a cornflakes box!&#13; I also became frustrated with the sanitising of the sport and how attempts were made to quash any kind of individuality, which is why larger-than-life characters are sadly missing from the sport these days.  10&#13; A Challenge of a Different Kind: 2008&#13; AT THE START OF 2008, THERE WAS MORE THAN JUST F1 ON my mind when what Ann and I were fearing came true. While I was away testing at Jerez in southern Spain, Ann detected our dog Milo had developed a chesty cough and sure enough, a visit to the vet and an X-ray revealed cancer in one of his lungs. Iâ€™d never really understood what it was like to form that special bond with an animal. In fact when I was younger I could never understand why my sister Leanne got so upset when she lost one of her pets. I barely paid any attention, never mind giving her any sympathy. Now it was happening to one of my own dogs. Milo and Shadow were a massive part of our home life; they were my mates and my training companions. I would seek them out as soon as I arrived home from a race and take them for a long walk, just the three of us, as I unwound from the stresses and demands of a Grand Prix weekend.&#13; Arriving home from the test and seeing Milo so overjoyed to see me ripped me apart. He didnâ€™t look ill but we knew his condition wasnâ€™t going to get any better. Totally oblivious to the reason she was there, he was still wagging his tail as she injected the drug. Iâ€™m not ashamed to admit I bawled my eyes out as I felt his head going heavy in my hands. His life slipped away and I remember I closed his big brown eyes. It was the worst day of my life. We had him cremated that afternoon and when we returned home, I took myself off for a long run in the dark with some of his ashes in a bag. I was saying goodbye to a mate.&#13; But the world of F1 stops for nobody and it was only a matter of a few days before the next F1 pre-season test beckoned.  11&#13; Yes I Can: 2009&#13; I HIT 2009 DETERMINED TO PROVE THAT I COULD PICK UP MY racing life where Iâ€™d left off.  12&#13; In High Places&#13; MY EARLY F1 YEARS TOOK ME TO BASE CAMP AT EVEREST. IN 2010 I would learn what it was like to attempt K2, which most serious climbers agree is the toughest mountain of them all.&#13; In his autobiography In High Places the great climber Dougal Haston said that the climber has to control self-doubt and fear; that climbing happens as much in the mind as in the battle of body against mountain, and sometimes the challenge seems unreasonable. Climbers start out quite often as a large group; they build camps at key positions on their route up the mountain; as they go higher the group gets smaller; in the end only the climbers capable of making the final push for the summit remain. And to make that push they need all the help they can get from the people behind them. Thatâ€™s how it was in the 2010 Formula 1 World Championship. By the end of the season we were down to four title contenders, two of us from the same team. The summit was within reach.&#13; Going into 2010 the Webber stocks were higher than they had ever been. The build-up to the collision.&#13; Point 1I have always tried to conduct myself properly as a professional sportsperson and a team player and I believe I continued to do so on Sunday, both on and off the track, in the aftermath of what happened.  13&#13; In Rare Air&#13; ALTHOUGH THE BAD SMELL FROM ISTANBUL STILL LINGERED, I re-signed with Red Bull Racing for the 2011 season just before the next race in Canada. My driving contract renewals and negotiations generally began around May or June of the preceding year and concluded, but were not always announced, by the end of July at the very latest. It was hard to swallow the fact that my teammate had come through on the line and won a photo-finish for the biggest prize in our sport, but I could handle that.&#13; If I was unhappy with anyone, it was with the Red Bull Racing management. When the kitchen got hot they didnâ€™t handle it very well, and thatâ€™s what got to me. I felt there were constant attempts to devalue what I had done, even when I was winning races for the team and leading the championship, and the jobâ€™s hard enough without having bricks put in your backpack and feeling that you are being constantly undermined. Unfortunately that feeling would only intensify through my final three years with Red Bull Racing. To many people, I know, it must seem as if the infamous â€˜Multi 21â€™ message in Malaysia in 2013 was the beginning of the end for me as a Red Bull Racing driver, but our relationship had started to disintegrate long before that unhappy day.&#13; Despite their protests to the contrary in 2010, I think recent history has proved that Red Bull were driven by a desire to produce the youngest-ever World Champion from the ranks of their Junior Driver Program. It would have been a failure for Helmut Marko otherwise.&#13; Every picture tells a story, as they say. Just how much this all meant to Marko was laid bare in one rather poignant shot of him, sitting on the top step of the podium in Abu Dhabi after going up to collect the teamâ€™s race-winning trophy and to share the podium with Seb. If you look closely at the apparent tears on Markoâ€™s face you have to wonder why he is crying. Is he remembering his friend Jochen Rindt, the sportâ€™s only posthumous World Champion? Or was it none of these reasons, but just the sheer relief at finally turning one of his Red Bull protÃ©gÃ©s into a World Champion?&#13; Whatever the reason, it was clearly an emotional moment for him and I have no problem with that. Ann understood too: she had been close to someone who had all the talent but never got the chance to fulfil it, Paul Warwick, who died at just 22. I never met Paul, of course, but I knew what happened to him was part of Annâ€™s reason for moving to Australia, and the foundation of her motivation to drive my career so passionately. After everything she and I had been through weâ€™d been beaten to the winning-post by a power structure that influenced the final decision, and nothing was ever going to change at Red Bull Racing.&#13; Still, none of this should detract from the job Seb did. I can say with absolute honesty that he is a better all-round F1 driver than I ever was. He has some sensational qualities. There were things he did and you just had to take your hat off to him. He has a computer-like approach to executing race weekends, and he was a Red Bull driver through and through, whereas the hardest thing for me at Red Bull Racing was to get some momentum going on my side. I always suspected Seb was just as much a pawn in the game as I had been and the pressure on him to deliver must have been immense. Iâ€™d just like to have had a crack at him 10 years earlier.&#13; One of the greatest challenges in Formula 1 is to have two drivers at the top of their game going for the same prize within the same team, especially when they arenâ€™t the only ones in the title race. This wasnâ€™t like 2014, when Mercedes had zero opposition in the championship, so once they had wrapped up the Constructorsâ€™ Championship they allowed their two drivers, Lewis Hamilton and Nico Rosberg, to slog it out for the Driversâ€™ Championship. In 2010 Seb and I were battling hard with Fernando, Lewis and Jenson for the ultimate prize, so I think everyone was a bit surprised that Red Bull Racing elected not to follow accepted F1 practice and throw their weight behind the man in the lead at the appropriate stage of the season.&#13; The well-respected F1 journalist Mark Hughes summed it up succinctly in his Autosport column immediately after Abu Dhabi 2010. Reflecting on my position, and the position Felipe Massa found himself in at Ferrari, he wrote:&#13; Red Bull, the Austrian soft drinks company, now has the perfect world champion for its marketing aims. Sebastian Vettel is nonetheless a sensationally good racing driver who has been caught in the middle of the conflict between those aims and those of the racing team that represents it. In both situations, Ferrariâ€™s and Red Bullâ€™s, it has left only a small space for the other driver. Not in terms of racing hardware or available resource, but in psychological terms. Neither can feel as wanted as the guy on the other side of the same garage â€¦ With Mark Webber itâ€™s as if feeling heâ€™s the underdog has brought out some of his greatest performances.&#13; To end on a wry note, as soon as Seb took the chequered flag in Abu Dhabi to win the title, the team handed out Sebastian Vettel, 2010 World Champion T-shirts. A couple of days later, assuming they would have produced similar T-shirts with my name on them, my office phoned the team to ask if it was possible to obtain a couple of the Mark Webber, 2010 World Champion shirts. After a bit of to-ing and fro-ing, the answer came back that they had already been thrown away! Maybe they could have saved on such wastage by producing a generic T-shirt that either driver could have worn â€“ as Mercedes did in 2014.&#13;

 14&#13; One Day Youâ€™ll Look Back&#13; ONE OF THE TOUGHEST THINGS ABOUT CLIMBING MOUNTAINS is that you have to come down again, whether or not you made it to the summit. And when you have come so close to the top of K2, itâ€™s a long way down, as I found out in the aftermath of Abu Dhabi 2010.&#13; At the start of 2009, after my accident in Tasmania, I had been in no real physical shape to take on the start of a new season. As the start of the 2011 campaign loomed, I wasnâ€™t mentally ready. My work-rate, my attention to detail just werenâ€™t what they should have been. I had geared myself to win the title in the previous season and then retire, partly to put an end to all of the negativity in my racing life, but also because there would have been a sense of having reached my ultimate destination. I would have been ready to come off the mountain. When that didnâ€™t happen, Iâ€™m willing to admit it knocked the stuffing out of me. The team were pretty good with me but the shift was obvious: it was as if I had lost the championship in 2010 by 400 points rather than losing it by a handful of points at the final round. As for me, the new season came too quickly and it was hard for me to find the motivation from within. I was finding it harder to keep the fire alight, and it was very tempting to stay at home and walk the dogs. But once I was back in the car the competitive juices started to flow again. It was all about me now: dig deep, show what I was made of off-track as well as in the cockpit. My target for 2011 was to get the absolute maximum out of myself. It was important to keep going back to the whole journey and see things in perspective.&#13; Adrianâ€™s latest brainchild, RB7, was launched in Valencia early in February of that year. It was the first Red Bull Racing car to incorporate KERS, which was reintroduced for that season; we had a new gizmo called the Drag Reduction System (DRS), supposedly to help drivers overtake on designated sections of each circuit; and, most importantly in my view, the sport had switched to Pirelli as sole suppliers of tyres.&#13; In the final years of my F1 career the Pirelli tyres posed the biggest challenge as far as I was concerned â€“ and at the same time they helped take Grand Prix racing in a direction I didnâ€™t particularly like. It would be a theme song throughout the year. There was a kind of long goodbye taking place.&#13; Mind you, someone clearly would have preferred it to be a short and snappy goodbye.  15&#13; Finishing Strongly&#13; BEFORE THE START OF MY FINAL SEASON IN F1, A FOUR-YEAR marathon came to an end: in December 2012 the last piece of metal was removed from my right leg, a 40-centimetre titanium rod that forced me to relax the Webber training regime for a brief period.  16&#13; In Another Cockpit: 2014 and Beyond&#13; ITâ€™S A CLOSED WORLD.&#13; But now the feeling of being shut in is totally different from what I knew in a Formula 1 car.
